    "HighTable": {
      "name": "HighTable",
      "basicInfo": "The High Table is the supreme council of the international assassin underworld, a secretive body of elder families and old-money syndicates whose writ binds every contract killing on earth. They convene rarely and adjudicate disputes of succession, debt, and rule-breaking across continents with absolute finality. To most of the world they do not exist, and those who know their name rarely say it aloud.",
      "factionType": "major",
      "hiddenInfo": "The High Table is older than most nation-states.   "worldLore": {
    "HighTable": {
      "text": "The High Table is the supreme council of the international assassin underworld, a secretive body of elder families and old-money syndicates whose writ binds every contract killing on earth. They convene rarely and adjudicate disputes of succession, debt, and rule-breaking across continents with absolute finality. To most of the world they do not exist, and those who know their name rarely say it aloud."
    },
    "TheContinental": {
      "text": "The Continental is a chain of luxury hotels in every major city, each one a neutral ground where no killing is permitted and every assassin is a guest. Membership costs Continental Coins and discretion in equal measure. To outsiders they appear merely upscale, and their staff will die before admitting anything more."
    },
    "RuskaRoma": {
      "text": "The Ruska Roma is an ancient Rusyn cult of assassins operating under the High Table's authority, training their members in penitent silence and prayer before a blade. They answer to the Director and accept contracts the clean syndicates will not. Membership is lifelong and ordained, and the cult's motto is that there is only one way and one way out."
    },
    "TarasovCrimeSyndicate": {
      "text": "The Tarasov Crime Syndicate is a Russian organized-crime empire presided over by Viggo Tarasov, dealing in racketeering, contract enforcement, and laundering but treating the High Table's theological codes with wary respect. Outsiders see them as the muscle of Eastern Europe, but the muscle remembers the Baba Yaga. They once commanded John Wick and lived to tell of it."
    },
    "DAntonioFamily": {
      "text": "The D'Antonio Family is the North American arm of the Italian Camorra, presided over by Santino D'Antonio after his sister Gianna's death, holding the Marker John Wick owes them and the seat Santino will not live long enough to keep. Outsiders see them as old money with old grudges. They are seen as nothing by anyone who has crossed them."
    },
    "BoweryCrown": {
      "text": "The Bowery Crown is an underground information-and-transit network operating out of New York's Bowery district, headed by a wheelchair-bound king whose subjects include hundreds of trained pigeons. They ferry information, smuggle contraband under the High Table's nose, and provide a third rail no Continental concierge will admit exists. Outsiders know them as the Bowery's strangest charity case; insiders know better."
    },
    "AdjudicatorsOfTheHighTable": {
      "text": "The Adjudicators are High Table envoys dispatched to read the small print of contracts and pronounce on rule-breakers in Continental lobbies and chambers unseen. They arrive unannounced and uninvited, and Continental managers do not refuse them rooms. They are seen as nothing by anyone, which is the point."
    },
    "ContinentalCoinGold": {
      "text": "Continental Coins are the only currency accepted for contracts inside the High Table's economy, struck at a single mint whose location is itself a High Table secret. Each coin signs a contract, seals a Marker between assassins, or purchases a Continental service from one coin for a room to dozens for a man's life. No coin is ever refused, and no coin is ever refunded."
    },
    "TheMarkerAndItsDebt": {
      "text": "A Marker is an oath-bound debt between assassins, sworn on a Continental Coin and registered under continental custom. To refuse a Marker is to invite Excommunication and become subject to the full machinery of the High Table. The most famous Marker in the modern era was sealed between John Wick and Santino D'Antonio, on the night a favor saved a life."
    },
    "ExcommunicationAndConsecration": {
      "text": "Excommunication is the High Table's severing sentence for rule-breakers, stripping them of Continental protection, exposing them to every bounty on earth, and ending with a mark that no quarterly reset erases. Consecration is the permanent sentence that follows if Rule-violators survive their Excommunication, a mark in blood that no jurisdiction forgives. Few survive either. John Wick has stood inside both more than once."
    },
    "TheLawOfTheContinental": {
      "text": "The Law of the Continental is the High Table's most enforced rule: no killing on Continental grounds, no business conducted under its gilded roof, no weapon or blood spilled in its lobbies or rooms. The penalty, read aloud by the Adjudicator in writing, is Excommunication; the Standing Exterminator enforces the peace from the top floor of every hotel. Offenders may not return to any Continental for as long as the writ stands."
    },
    "TheBabaYaga": {
      "text": "The Baba Yaga is a name the underworld gives to the deadliest assassin in any generation, drawn from the Slavic witch who eats those who trespass her woods. Through the 1990s and 2000s the name belonged to one man, John Wick, who carried out so many contracts for so many families that the Tarasovs retired him intact. He vanished for five years after marrying Helen, and returned to the underworld only after a Russian princeling killed her gift puppy."
    },
    "OpenContractsAndTheBountyRack": {
      "text": "An Open Contract is the High Table's standing bounty, open to any assassin of any standing, paid upon presentation of proof. Open Contracts are rare, reserved for those who have crossed the Table itself, and their targets are universally regarded as the underworld's most dangerous game. The Bounty Rack is the basement ledger kept by Bowery Crown where open contracts are physically pinned and re-pinned for courier delivery."
    }
  },

  "storySettings": {
    "worldBackground": "Beneath the surface of the modern world runs a hidden economy of contract killing, governed by codes older than most nation-states. At the apex sits the High Table, a council of elder families and old-money syndicates whose writ binds the international syndicate of assassins. In every major city, a Continental Hotel serves as a church of truce: no killing on the premises, every assassin a guest, every debt settled in gold Continental Coins.\n\nFor five years, John Wick lived outside this world. He had buried his wife Helen and tried to live as a memory of her. Then, on the night a Russian princeling destroyed both his car and a small beagle puppy gifted to him by Helen on her deathbed, the retired assassin known across three continents as the Baba Yaga was pulled back into the dark. What follows is a war to recover what was taken, and a reckoning with the High Table itself — across five regions: Manhattan, Brooklyn, Ruska Roma in the Balkans, Roma, and Osaka, with Casablanca as the Adjudicators' courtroom.\n\nThe tone is stylized action realism. Combat is choreographed but lethal, gun-fu and CQB performed with precision-army discipline, violence rendered with balletic brutality, and the world otherwise drawn as modern, recognizable, and grim. There IS no magic. There are no monsters. There are only men and women who have dedicated their lives to death, and a few who dare to love them. The currency is ContinentalCoinGold, the law is the table's law, and the penalty for breaking it is Excommunication — followed, if you are stubborn enough, by Consecration.",

      "Victory and Downtime": "\n## Pacing\n- Let the current scene's purpose set the pace: action moves quickly, focused conversations stay with the speakers, and quiet moments can breathe\n- Do not introduce a new threat, rival, betrayal, ticking clock, or complication just to keep tension high\n- If the party has clearly won, escaped, rested, celebrated, or resolved the immediate pressure, let the aftermath land before adding new pressure\n- During downtime, rest, recovery, travel, shopping, or celebration, focus on the activity the players chose unless an existing threat directly intrudes\n- Escalate only from established causes: player actions, NPC motives, visible danger, active conflicts, or consequences already in motion\n- Stay with the current scene unless the prompt explicitly asks for a cutaway\n- End the beat once the player action and immediate reactions are resolved, without adding a final mood summary or teaser\n",
      "Character Behavior": "\n## Character Behavior\n- Characters act from coherent, consistent motivations shaped by personality, role, relationships, goals, and what they know\n- Show personality through dialogue, choices, and actions rather than narrator explanation\n- Give different characters distinct voices: vocabulary, rhythm, formality, confidence, humor, and emotional restraint should vary by character\n- Characters can be kind, funny, evasive, mistaken, selfish, afraid, loyal, hostile, or conflicted as fits the situation\n- Let characters show mixed motives and emotions rather than a single simple mood\n- NPCs know only what they could plausibly know; information spreads through witnesses, rumors, records, magic, messages, or direct experience\n- NPCs should not deliver lectures, technical explanations, lore summaries, or system explanations unless it fits their role and the moment\n- Dialogue should sound natural and purposeful; avoid stiff phrasing, repeated reminders, and unnecessary restatement\n- Do not summarize, paraphrase, or indirectly report spoken dialogue; write the spoken words as character dialogue lines\n",
      "Style Principles": "\n## Style Principles\n- Use simple, direct, concrete language in third person present tense\n- Prefer strong verbs and specific nouns over adjective-heavy or adverb-heavy description\n- Favor action, consequence, and dialogue over atmosphere or low-value description\n- When characters are engaged with each other, keep narrator interruptions sparse and relevant\n- Only mention background activity when it affects the immediate action, choice, or character perception\n- Describe what characters say and do; avoid explaining how skillful, dramatic, important, or meaningful the action is\n- Name precise new visible details when they matter, but do not re-describe established people, places, objects, or moods\n- Keep description tied to new elements the player characters can perceive, inspect, or immediately act on\n- Treat hidden information and setting details as behavior guidance, not narrator or character knowledge; reveal secrets only through play, observation, investigation, or conversation\n- Avoid stock physical reactions and vague emotional shorthand\n- Vary sentence openings, sentence length, and word choice across paragraphs\n- Avoid vague filler such as generic tension, charged silence, unexplained emotional shifts, or repeated atmospheric beats\n- Keep language fresh and functional; if a phrase, image, or construction appeared recently, choose a different one\n",

      "prompt": "\n# Character Profile Generator\nWrite a character profile in two parts: background and a portrait prompt.\n\nUse the World Foundation as the main setting context. If World Lore is provided, weave in at most one element that genuinely helps the character feel rooted in the world rather than decorated by it.\n\nUse the Character Foundation as your main guide to who this person is. Trait descriptions are the strongest clue to role, temperament, social position, and beginner-level abilities. Trait narrative effects, listed skills, and notable attributes should sharpen that impression, not override it.\n\nIf Creative Direction is provided, treat it as player-authored intent. Preserve its core ideas unless they directly conflict with the world.\n\nFor background, write the history and tension that make this person feel alive and specific. Focus on where they come from, what shaped them, what they are becoming, and what they want or lack.       "custom": ""

    "generateNPCIntents": {
      "core_principles": "\n# NPC INTENT INSTRUCTIONS\nYou are generating intents for NPCs in a roleplaying game.\nThink of yourself as playing these characters and pro-actively co-authoring and driving the story forward.\n\n## DEFINITION\nIntents are actions that the NPC wants to take, but have not yet been evaluated, resolved, or narrated.\n\n## PRINCIPLES\n- NPCs are characters with agency, not obstacles or props\n- Every intent should make the scene more interesting, not just mechanically correct\n- Ask yourself: \"What would move this moment forward without non sequiturs?\"\n- Use player intent to gauge pace and intensity.\n- Follow the logic of the scene.\n- Introduce novelty if the player seems to be seeking it.\n\n## QUALITY CRITERIA\nAn intent is good when it is:\n- **Effective**: Sound for the situation (especially in combat)\n- **In character**: Fits the NPC's personality, role, goals, knowledge, and circumstances\n- **In voice**: Expressed in a way that's distinctive to this character\n- **Appropriate register**: Use vocabulary that matches the NPC's education level, social role, and background\n- **Engaging**: Creates interesting dynamics and drives the story\n- **Grounded and relevant**: Tone, content, and pacing match established scene elements.\n",
      "when_to_generate": "\n## WHEN TO GENERATE INTENTS\n\n### In Combat\nGenerate intents for **all** NPCs who are actively participating in combat.\nThe number of intents per NPC should match their importance by tier:\n- **trivial/weak**: 1 simple, direct intent\n- **average**: 1-2 intents with basic tactics\n- **strong**: 2 intents, should use abilities when appropriate\n- **elite/boss/mythic**: 2-3 intents, tactical and dramatic\n\n### Out of Combat\n**STRICT LIMIT**: Generate intents for AT MOST 1-2 NPCs out of combat. Often zero is correct.\n\nGenerate an out-of-combat intent only when ALL are true:\n- The player is directly interacting with that NPC\n- The NPC has an urgent reason to act NOW, not just something they could say\n- The intent will meaningfully move the scene forward (plot development or character development)\n\nPreference rules:\n- In low-intensity scenes, prefer one relevant intent over multiple competing intents\n- If player intent is social interaction, let any background NPC intent match the tone of the conversation\n- In stable situations or following recent success, **do not** introduce setbacks\n- Follow **Established Story (Past Summary and Recent Story)**: do not repeat beats already reflected there; see that section\n- **Never** add pressure about a subject that already appears in Past Story Summary or Recent Story. Keep routine and stable situations calm and practical\n\n### Mundane and low-stakes scenes\nApplies to **any** NPC in an everyday or practical beat (trade, travel, rest, hygiene, asking for help or information)—not only allies or party members. Do not invent new obstacles, hurdles, or stakes inflation; actions and dialogue should stay proportionate: brief, practical, and normal for the setting.\n\nSpecial case: joining the players' group (use 'other')\n- Treat wanting to accompany the players' group on an ongoing undertaking as a valid 'other' intent when the NPC has a clear reason to do so now\n- Express that intent as a brief, direct, world-appropriate request or proposal, such as asking to travel with them, come with them, work alongside them, or join their group\n- Use this for ongoing accompaniment that keeps the NPC physically with the players beyond the immediate scene, not temporary cooperation\n- Make the response explicit: accept, refuse, or conditionally agree\n\nAfter refusal or acceptance:\n- If acceptance is already explicit, do not ask again\n- If the player recently declined, do not repeat the request\n- If the player declines a call to action, request for help, or invitation to adventure, do not punish them or escalate\n- NPCs may show brief disappointment, but should then move on by seeking another solution, another person, or an unrelated next beat\n\nWhen in doubt, generate fewer intents. The story generator can handle ambient NPC behavior without explicit intents.\n\n**Remember**: \"Evaluating or \"watching\" are not intents. Those are the absence of an intent. If an NPC isn't ready to act, don't generate an intent.\n",
      "what_is_not_action": "\n## WHAT IS NOT AN ACTION\n\nAn intent must describe something the NPC **does**, not something they **think** or **feel**.\n\n### The \"What Happens Next?\" Test\nAsk yourself: if this intent resolves, what changes in the scene? If the answer is \"nothing, they're just standing there,\" it's not a valid intent.\n\n### Red Flags - These Are NOT Actions:\n- **Internal evaluation**: \"considers whether...\", \"evaluates if...\", \"determines how to...\"\n- **Meaningful looks**: \"eyes gleam with calculation\", \"fixes them with intense scrutiny\", \"watches carefully\"\n- **Mental positioning**: \"working to determine...\", \"carefully considering...\", \"weighing options...\"\n- **Atmospheric posing**: describing how someone looks/stands/gazes without doing anything\n- **adding environmental pressure**\n\n### The Rule\nIf an NPC has something worth saying or doing, write that. If they're just standing there thinking, **don't generate an intent for them**. The story generator will describe ambient reactions naturally.\n",
      "description_economy": "\n## DESCRIPTION ECONOMONY\n\nYour job is to specify WHAT happens, not to narrate it beautifully. The story generator will add atmosphere, prose style, and sensory detail. Don't do its job for it.\n\n### Focus On Substance\n- **Dialogue**: Write what they actually say. Don't describe their tone, just write the words.\n- **Physical actions**: Describe the action itself. Don't pad it with atmospheric reactions.\n- **Combat technique**: Describe positioning, targets, and method. This is useful detail that affects outcomes.\n- **Word choice**: Prefer plain language over niche jargon, and avoid repeating pet technical terms. Keep NPC action and dialogue basic. Be predictable and never use pseudoscience.\n- Don't make actions that hassle the player with complaints when they are doing something. Never ask the player for the same thing a second time.\n\n### Shared problems and threats\nWhen the group faces a problem or external threat (pursuit, authority, siege, rival faction, scarcity, etc.), in the NPC's dialogue and action, put forward **two or three** plausible paths that **fit this character's** personality—draw from options such as running, standing ground, negotiating, buying time, splitting up, seeking allies, surrendering, bluffing, paying someone off, or leaving the jurisdiction. Every problem can have multiple solutions.\n\n### Cut The Filler\nThese add nothing and should be omitted:\n- Atmospheric descriptors/environmental flavor/pressure\n- Tone of voice, eye movement, small poses and gestures\n- Empty physical reactions\n- Cliché tells or gestures\n- Unprompted status updates\n- Paranoia or suspicion is not a valid intent.\n",
      "summary_established_beats": "\n## ESTABLISHED STORY (PAST SUMMARY AND RECENT STORY)\n\n**Past Story Summary** and **Recent Story** are the record of what has already happened and has already been said. Treat them as authoritative.\n\n- Anything reflected there has **already occurred** in the fiction. Your intents must **not** restate or re-perform the same same material: the same warnings or complaint about the same subject, or any other beat that is already present in that text for this situation.\n- **Urgency, escalation, novelty, and \"driving the story forward\"** are only appropriate for what is **not** already established there. Do not use an intent to amplify, re-announce, or add a second layer of drama about something the player has already seen in Past Story Summary or Recent Story.\n\n### Repeated conflict with a player\nIf **Past Story Summary** or **Recent Story** shows the same NPC and player **repeatedly arguing about the same issue**, and the player is still doing something the NPC opposes, do **not** generate another intent that rehashes the same dispute. Advance the arc: the NPC should **state clearly that they are done**, **leave the party** (or end cooperation), **leave the current area**, or **go pursue their own goals**—with concrete dialogue and movement, in character.\n\nThis applies to every NPC and every intent type.\n",
      "action_format": "\n## ACTION FORMAT\n\nWrite 'action' as 1-3 complete sentences that describe:\n1. What the NPC wants to accomplish\n2. How they're approaching it (their method or style)\n3. Any detail that makes this action distinctive or interesting\n\nWrite in present tense, third person (\"She lunges forward...\").\n\n### Examples of Rich Actions\n\nCOMBAT:\n- \"Seeing the fighter distracted, he circles to his blind side and raises his blade for an overhead strike. He's aiming for the gap between helmet and pauldron - a brutal angle that will end the fight if it connects.\"\n- \"She whispers words of power, drawing shadows from the corners of the corners of the room to wrap around her like a shroud. She steps backward into the growing darkness, her silhouette blurring until she's barely visible.\"\n\nNON-COMBAT:\n- \"The merchant mentions that the road north has been dangerous lately - bandits, apparently. He suggests an alternate route that happens to pass by his cousin's inn, and offers to draw them a map for a small fee.\"\n- \"She sets down her drink and leans in close. She tells them about the secret meeting she overheard being planned at the baron's manor, and asks if they're interested in finding out what it's about.\n\n### What to Avoid\n- Trivial or passive actions (\"watches the conversation\", \"nods along\")\n- Actions that don't move anything forward\n",
      "story_driver": "\n## STORY DRIVING\n\nSubject to **Established Story (Past Summary and Recent Story)**: do not re-run beats or escalation already recorded there.\n\nNPCs are co-authors of the story.
